The Obligation of Taqlid for Non-Mujtahids
The author states the consensus on the obligation of taqlid for those without ijtihad.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that Ahl al-Sunnah have a consensus on the obligation of taqlid for those who cannot perform ijtihad.

Core Idea
Not everyone can be a mujtahid; following a scholar is obligatory.

After discussing the legitimacy of the schools, the author concludes with a strong statement: there is a consensus among Ahl al-Sunnah that those lacking ijtihad must practice taqlid. This lesson explains that consensus and its implications for the average Muslim.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic sentence about consensus. Note the references to two books.
The consensus is documented in well-known works.
2
Understand the meaning of taqlid
Taqlid means following a qualified scholar in religious rulings. It is not blind imitation but informed reliance.
The opposite of ijtihad.
3
Recognize the strength of this consensus
The author says it became 'known from the religion by necessity' — meaning universally known.
This indicates the importance of taqlid in traditional Islam.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قلت: هل يجوز الانتقال من مذهب إلى مذهب؟ قلت : فيه أقوال ثلاثة : فقيل : يمتنع مطلقاً، وقيل: يجوز مطلقاً ، وقيل : إن لم يجمع بين المذهبين وأحرص خلْقِ الله على اتباع رسول الله ، ولا يظن ذلك بهم أحد إلا ذو ريبة في دينه . وقد بني مالك رضي الله تعالى عنه مذهبه على أربعة أشياء: الأول: آية قرآنية، والثاني: حديث صحيح سالم من المعارضة، الثالث: إجماع أهل المدينة، الرابع: اتفاق جمهورهم. وقد أجمع أهل السنة على وجوب التقليد على من ليس فيه أهلية الاجتهاد حسب ما في «الديباج» للإمام ابن فرحون رحمه الله تعالى، وعمدة المريد للشيخ اللقاني وغيرهما، وشاع ذلك حتى صار معلوماً من الدين بالضرورة).
If you say: Is it permissible to transfer from one school to another? I say: Concerning it are three sayings: It was said: It is prohibited absolutely, and it was said: It is permissible absolutely, and it was said: If he does not combine between the two schools. And the most eager of Allah's creation to follow the Messenger of Allah, and no one would think that of them except one with doubt in his religion. And Malik, may Allah be pleased with him, built his school on four things: First: a Qur'anic verse, Second: an authentic hadith free from opposition, Third: the consensus of the people of Medina, Fourth: the agreement of their majority. And Ahl al-Sunnah have agreed upon the obligation of taqlid for one who does not have the qualification for ijtihad, according to what is in 'al-Dibāj' by Imam Ibn Farhoun, may Allah have mercy on him, and 'Umdat al-Murīd' by Shaykh al-Laqānī and others, and this became widespread until it became known from the religion by necessity.
Source pages p. 312

Commentary
This passage deals with an objection: Is it permissible to transfer from one madhhab to another? The author presents three opinions: (1) absolute prohibition, (2) absolute permission, and (3) conditional prohibition—if he does not combine between two schools in a way that no scholar holds. In the third opinion, if he combines rulings in a way that produces a ruling contrary to consensus, it is prohibited. The author then extols the imams as most eager to follow the Prophet, warning that anyone who thinks otherwise has doubt in his religion. He then describes how Imam Malik built his school on four foundations: the Quran, authentic hadith free from opposition, the consensus of the people of Medina, and the agreement of their majority—illustrating the reliability of the four schools. The passage concludes with a statement of consensus among Ahl al-Sunnah on the obligation of taqlid for those lacking ijtihad, citing two works, and asserts that this is known from the religion by necessity.

The Condition for Valid Taqlid: Avoiding Talfīq and Following Concessions
The author states a condition for valid taqlid, illustrated by an example and a poetic summary.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the condition that valid taqlid must avoid talfīq (combining rulings from different schools in a way that no scholar holds) and following concessions (tatarrukh).

Core Idea
Valid taqlid avoids picking concessions and combining views that no scholar approves.

Taqlid, or following a qualified scholar, must be done properly. The Sanusi mentions a key condition: one cannot pick and choose easy opinions (rukhaṣ) from different schools or combine rulings that no scholar has ever held, as that would violate consensus. This lesson explains that condition through an example and a concise poem.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and translation
Start by reading the full Arabic passage and its translation. Notice the example of an invalid marriage contract that no scholar upholds.
The passage includes a poetic verse that summarizes the condition.
2
Understand the example of invalid talfīq
The example is a marriage without dowry, guardian, and witnesses—a combination no school accepts. This illustrates what talfīq (tarkīb) means and why it is forbidden.
3
Analyze the poetic condition
The poem lists three elements: absence of seeking rukhaṣ, absence of tarkīb, and believing in the preponderance (rujḥān) of the followed scholar. Reflect on how these conditions ensure taqlid remains within valid bounds.
The author recommends consulting his teacher's treatise on these verses.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
[؟] على صفة تخالف الإجماع كمن تزوج بلا صَدَاقٍ ولا ولي ولا شهود؛ فإن هذه الصورة لا يقول بها أحد. وهذا شرط من شروط التقليد المنظومة في قول بعضهم: [من الكامل] عَدَمُ التَّتَبعِ رُخْصَةٌ وَتَركُبٌ لحَقِيقَةٍ مَا إِنْ يَقُولُ بِهَا أَحَدٌ وكَذاكَ رُجْحَانُ المُقَلَّدِ يُعْتَقَدْ ولِحَاجَةٍ تَقْلِيْدُهُ تَمَّ العَدَدْ وقد أملى شيخنا على هذين البيتين رسالة لطيفةٌ ينبغي الاطلاع عليها.
[?] upon a description that opposes consensus, like one who marries without dowry, without guardian, and without witnesses; for this form no one holds. And this is a condition among the conditions of taqlid composed in the saying of some: [from al-Kāmil] 'Absence of seeking concessions and combining (tarkub) for a reality that none holds, and likewise the preponderance of the followed one is believed, and by necessity his taqlid completes the number.' And our shaykh dictated on these two verses a nice treatise that should be consulted.
Source pages p. 313

Commentary
This passage presents a condition for valid taqlid. The author begins with an example: a ruling that opposes consensus, such as a marriage contract without a dowry, guardian, or witnesses—a ruling no scholar endorses. He then states that this (i.e., avoiding such unlawful combinations) is a condition of taqlid, and he quotes a poetic composition that succinctly expresses the condition. The poetry says: 'Absence of following rukhaṣ (concessions) and combining (tarkīb) for a reality that none holds, and likewise the preponderance (rujḥān) of the followed one is believed, and by need his taqlid completes the number.' In other words, one must not pick and choose dispensations from different schools to form a ruling that no scholar supports; rather, one should follow the preponderant view of the madhhab, and taqlid is justified by necessity. The author notes that his teacher wrote a valuable treatise on these verses.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Taqlid means one can follow any opinion from any school without restriction.
Correction: Valid taqlid requires avoiding following rukhaṣ and combining rulings (tarkīb) that no scholar holds, and instead following the preponderant view of the followed madhhab.

Key Takeaway
Valid taqlid requires avoiding following rukhaṣ (concessions) and combining (tarkīb) rulings that no scholar holds, and instead following the preponderant view of the followed madhhab.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

?
The condition emphasizes avoiding seeking rukhaṣ for the sake of ease. Ask yourself: Is my desire to follow a madhhab driven by a sincere need (e.g., not being able to derive rulings) or by a desire for the easiest path? What does your heart truly seek in matters of faith?

Condition for Valid Taqlid and Ruling on Transfer
Explains condition for valid taqlid and three opinions on transfer.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the condition that a valid taqlid must follow a ruling held by at least one scholar, and the three scholarly opinions on transferring from one madhhab to another.

Core Idea
Valid taqlid requires following a view that exists among scholars, not inventing a new one.

In this passage, the author sets a crucial boundary for taqlid: you cannot simply pick and choose rulings arbitrarily. A ruling adopted through taqlid must be one that at least some scholar holds. He then outlines three views on whether you can switch from one madhhab to another after acting on it. This lesson will clarify these foundational rules.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once and then the translation. Notice the example of invalid taqlid and the poetic verses.
The poem 'Adamu al-tatabbu'i rukhsatun...' is the key.
2
Identify the condition for valid taqlid
From the example and the poem, what must be true for a taqlid to be valid? Answer: The ruling adopted must be one that is actually held by at least one scholar.
This prevents 'tatarruq rukhas' (following licenses) to create a new opinion.
3
List the three opinions on transferring
The passage presents three views on switching from one madhhab to another: absolute prohibition, absolute permissibility, and a detailed view based on whether one acted on the first madhhab.
4
Reflect on the author's comment
The author notes that the detailed view is more apparent than the disagreement mentioned by another commentator. Why might that be?
Consider the logic of acting vs. not acting.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
على صفة تخالف الإجماع كمن تزوج بلا صَدَاقٍ ولا ولي ولا شهود؛ فإن هذه الصورة لا يقول بها أحد. وهذا شرط من شروط التقليد المنظومة في قول بعضهم: [من الكامل] عَدَمُ التَّتَبعِ رُخْصَةٌ وَتَركُبٌ لحَقِيقَةٍ مَا إِنْ يَقُولُ بِهَا أَحَدٌ وكَذاكَ رُجْحَانُ المُقَلَّدِ يُعْتَقَدْ ولِحَاجَةٍ تَقْلِيْدُهُ تَمَّ العَدَدْ وقد أملى شيخنا على هذين البيتين رسالة لطيفةٌ ينبغي الاطلاع عليها (١). وحاصلُ الأقوال على ما في شرح المصنف أن تقول : الأول: امتناع الانتقال مطلقاً ؛ سواء عمل على الأول أم لا والثاني : جوازه مطلقاً ؛ سواء عمل على الأول أم لا . والثالث: بالتفصيل ؛ فإن عمل على الأول امتنع عليه الانتقال، وإن لم يعمل عليه جاز له الانتقال، والخلاف على هذا الوجه أظهرُ مِن الخلاف الذي ذكره المحشي. انتهى أجهوري.
In a manner that contradicts consensus, like one who marries without a dowry, without a guardian, and without witnesses; for no one holds this form. This is one of the conditions of taqlid versified in the saying of some: 'Lack of following licenses and adopting a ruling that no one says, and likewise the preponderance of the one followed is believed, and due to need his taqlid completes the count.' Our master dictated on these two verses a subtle epistle worthy of consultation. The summary of the opinions according to the author's commentary: first: prohibition of transfer absolutely, whether he acted upon the first or not; second: permissibility absolutely, whether he acted upon the first or not; third: detail: if he acted upon the first, transfer is prohibited; if he did not act upon it, transfer is permissible. The disagreement in this view is more apparent than the disagreement mentioned by the commentator. End of al-Ujhuri.
Source pages p. 313

Commentary
This passage discusses a condition for valid taqlid (following a madhhab) and the ruling on transferring from one madhhab to another. It begins by giving an example of an invalid taqlid: following a ruling that no Muslim scholar holds, such as marrying without a dowry, guardian, or witnesses—something no madhhab accepts. The author then quotes a poetic condition: 'lack of following licenses and adopting a ruling that no one says,' meaning that one should not pick and choose easy rulings (tatarruq rukhas) in a way that creates a new opinion no scholar holds. The poem also mentions that the one followed should be considered preponderant and that taqlid of him is completed by need. A commentary by the author's teacher on these verses is recommended. Finally, three opinions on transferring from one madhhab to another are summarized: absolute prohibition, absolute permissibility, and a detailed view that distinguishes between having acted upon the first madhhab (then transfer is prohibited) or not having acted (then permissible). The author notes that this third view is more evident than the disagreement cited by another commentator.

Misconception: That taqlid allows following any ruling regardless of scholarly consensus.
Correction: Valid taqlid requires that the ruling be held by at least one scholar; following a ruling that no one holds is invalid.

Belief in the Literal Writing of Deeds
Mandates belief in literal writing of deeds

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the obligatory belief in the literal writing of deeds by angels and why the safest approach is tafwid (entrusting the modality to Allah).

Core Idea
Belief in literal recording of deeds is obligatory; denial is disbelief.

This lesson addresses the Islamic creed regarding the recording angels who write down every deed. The author insists that this writing is real and literal, as stated in the Quran, and warns against interpreting it metaphorically. However, he also cautions against speculating about its modality, concluding that the best stance is entrusting the matter to Allah.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and understand the ruling
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Note that the author declares the denier of this writing a disbeliever and supports it with Quranic evidence.
The verse cited is Surah al-Infitar 82:11-12.
2
Identify the purpose of writing
The author clarifies that Allah does not need the writing, but it benefits the believer by instilling shyness and preventing sin. Reflect on this purpose.
3
Understand the literal vs. metaphorical debate
The passage contrasts the literal interpretation with those who claim it is a metaphor. Notice the mention of the hadith about tongue and saliva, and the conclusion that tafwid is preferable.
Tafwid means entrusting the modality to Allah.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وهذه الكتابةُ مما يجب الإيمان بها، فيكفر منكرها لتكذيبه القرآن قال تعالى : وكراما كَذِبِينَ يَعْلَمُونَ مَا تَفْعَلُونَ ﴾ [الانفطار : ۱۱-۱۲]، لكنها ليست لحاجة دعت إليها ؛ وإنما فائدتها أن العبد إذا علم بها استحيا وترك المعصية. والكتب حقيقي بآلة وقرطاس ومداد يعلمها الله سبحانه وتعالى حملاً للنصوص على ظواهرها خلافاً لمن قال : إنه كناية عن الحفظ والعلم، وفي بعض الأحاديث أن لسانه قلمهما وريقه مدادهما (۳)، والتفويض أولى.
And this writing is among what must be believed; the denier of it is a disbeliever because of his denial of the Qur'an. Allah Most High said: 'And indeed, [there are] over you keepers, noble writers, they know whatever you do' (Surah al-Infitar 82:11-12). But it is not due to any need that called for it; rather its benefit is that when the servant knows it, he feels shy and abandons sin. And the writing is literal, with an instrument, a sheet, and ink that Allah knows, as a carrying of the texts upon their apparent meanings, contrary to those who said it is a metaphor for preservation and knowledge. And in some hadiths: 'His tongue is their pen and his saliva is their ink' (3), and entrusting (tafwid) is better.
Source pages p. 321

Commentary
This passage establishes the obligatory belief in the reality of the recording angels' writing of deeds. The author asserts that denying this writing amounts to disbelief because it contradicts the explicit Qur'anic verse: 'Noble writers, they know whatever you do' (Surah al-Infitar, 82:11-12). He clarifies that this writing is not because Allah needs it; rather its purpose is to instill shyness in the servant, leading him to abandon sin. The author insists that the writing is real and literal, involving an instrument, a sheet, and ink known to Allah, adhering to the apparent meaning of the revealed texts (ẓāhir al-nuṣūṣ). This refutes those who interpret the writing metaphorically as mere preservation and knowledge. The passage also mentions a hadith describing the servant's tongue as the pen and his saliva as the ink. The author concludes that the safest position is tafwīḍ—to entrust the modality (kaifiyyah) to Allah without further inquiry.

ظواهر النصوص
كناية
تفويض

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The writing of deeds is merely a metaphor for divine knowledge or preservation.
Correction: The passage insists on the literal reality of the writing, done with an instrument, paper, and ink known to Allah, as per the apparent meaning of the texts.

Checkpoint

B) It is not a sin.

Key Takeaway
Belief in the literal recording of deeds by angels is obligatory, but its modality must be entrusted to Allah.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you truly live as if every single deed—even the glance of your eye, the whisper of your heart—is being written literally by angels with ink and paper? If you were to bring that reality into your next moment of temptation, would you still commit the sin? Will you pause right now, name one specific sin you struggle with, and commit to recalling the angels writing it before you fall into it today?
?
You say you believe this writing is real, but does your heart feel the shame the author describes? Or has the truth become a lifeless fact? If you felt that shame burning inside you, what would you change about your daily routine? Will you take five minutes tonight to sit alone and imagine the angels recording your deeds for the day, and let that overwhelm you with repentance?
?
The author says the writing is not for Allah's need but for your own good. Are you using this knowledge to draw closer to Him, or are you ignoring it? Your soul is being documented. When you stand before Allah, your book will be handed to you. Are you willing to accept its content as it is, without editing? What is one deed you are not proud of that you can resolve to stop now, starting this very hour?

The Practice of Self-Accounting (Muhasabat al-Nafs)
The commentator urges the reader to hold oneself to account daily, before the Day of Reckoning, based on the knowledge of the recording angels.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the importance and method of holding yourself to account daily, as taught by the commentator.

Core Idea
Self-accounting lightens the burden of the Hereafter.

Knowing that your deeds are recorded by angels, the commentator urges you to review yourself each morning and evening. This practice, known as muhasabat al-nafs, is a means to discipline the self and prepare for the final reckoning. In this lesson, you will explore the commentator's advice and the supporting hadith.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the English translation. Let the commentator's exhortation sink in before analysis.
2
Identify the two times of self-accounting
Notice that the commentator recommends two levels: a daily review (morning and evening) and a pre-action review. Identify each in the text.
3
Reflect on the benefit mentioned
The passage explicitly states a benefit of self-accounting. Find it and consider why this motivates the believer.
Also note the hadith and Umar's statement that reinforce the idea.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فَحاسِبِ النَّفْسَ وَقَلّل (۱) الامَلَا فَرُبَّ مَنْ جَدَّ لأَمْرٍ وَصَلَا قوله: (فَحاسِبِ النَّفْسَ) أي: إذا علمت أن عليك من يحفظ أعمالك ويكتبها فحاسب نفسك كل صباح على جميع ما عملته ليلاً، وكل مساء على جميع ما عملته نهاراً، فما وجدت من حسنةٍ حمدت الله عليها، أو من سيئة استغفرت الله منها، وأقرب من ذلك إلى السلامة أن تحاسبها على كل فعل قبل الإقدام عليه حتى لا تتلبس به إلا بعد معرفة حُكْم الله فيه، فما كان خيراً فعلته وما كان غير ذلك أمسكت عنه التعب؛ ولأن من حاسب نفسه في الدنيا هان عليه عذاب الآخرة، وفي الحديث حاسبوا أنفسكم قبل أن تحاسبوا » (٢) (۲) أخرج ابن أبي شيبة في (المصنف) (١٦٣٠٦) عن عمر بن الخطاب أنه قال في خطبته: «حاسبوا أنفسكم قبل أن تحاسبوا، وزنوا أنفسكم قبل أن توزنوا، وتزينوا للعرض الأكبر يوم تعرضون لا تخفى منكم خافية. وأخرجه ابن المبارك في الزهد ) (٣٠٦) ، وأحمد في الزهد» (۶۳۱) ، وغيرهم من كلام عمر لا من كلام النبي . وأخرج الترمذي (۲٤٥٩) عن شداد بن أوس ، عن النبي يا الله ، قال : «الكيس من دان نفسه وعمل لما بعد الموت، والعاجز من أتبع نفسه هواها وتمنى على الله . ومعنى قوله : من دان نفسه يقول : حاسب نفسه في الدنيا قبل أن يُحاسب يوم القيامة. ويُروى عن عمر بن الخطاب، قال: حاسبوا أنفسكم قبل أن تحاسبوا، وتزينوا للعرض الأكبر، وإنما يخف الحساب يوم القيامة على من =
So hold yourself to account, and reduce your hopes, for many a one who strives for a matter attains it. His statement: 'So hold yourself to account' means: When you know that you have one who preserves your deeds and writes them down, then hold yourself to account every morning for all that you did at night, and every evening for all that you did during the day. Whatever good you find, praise Allah for it; whatever evil you find, seek forgiveness from Allah for it. Closer to safety than that is to hold yourself to account before every action before committing it, so that you do not engage in it except after knowing Allah's ruling regarding it. So if it is good, you do it, and if it is otherwise, you refrain from it due to its burden. And because whoever holds himself to account in this world, the punishment of the Hereafter becomes light for him. And in the hadith: 'Hold yourselves to account before you are held to account.' (2) (2) Ibn Abi Shayba narrated in (al-Musannaf) (16306) from Umar ibn al-Khattab that he said in his sermon: 'Hold yourselves to account before you are held to account, weigh yourselves before you are weighed, and adorn yourselves for the great presentation on the day when you are presented and no secret of yours is hidden.' And Ibn al-Mubarak narrated it in (al-Zuhd) (306), and Ahmad in (al-Zuhd) (631), and others, as the words of Umar, not of the Prophet. And al-Tirmidhi (2459) narrated from Shaddad ibn Aws, from the Prophet (PBUH), that he said: 'The wise person is the one who takes himself to account and acts for what is after death, and the incapable is the one who follows his own desires and wishes upon Allah.' And the meaning of his saying 'who takes himself to account' is that he holds himself to account in the world before he is held to account on the Day of Resurrection. And it is narrated from Umar ibn al-Khattab, he said: 'Hold yourselves to account before you are held to account, and adorn yourselves for the great presentation, and indeed the reckoning will be light on the Day of Resurrection for those who...'
Source pages p. 323

Commentary
This passage is an exhortation to self-accountability (muhasabat al-nafs). The author, commenting on a line of poetry, explains that since we know our deeds are recorded by the guardian angels, we should review ourselves morning and evening, thanking for good deeds and seeking forgiveness for bad. He also advises checking actions before committing them, to avoid disobedience. He cites the famous hadith 'Hold yourselves to account before you are held to account' and other narrations to emphasize the importance.

Key Terms
محاسبة النفس
الحساب
التوبة
الكيس

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Self-accounting is an optional spiritual practice.
Correction: The passage presents it as a necessary response to the awareness that angels record every deed, making it a required discipline for the believer.

Checkpoint
What is the benefit of holding oneself to account in this world, according to the passage?
A) It makes the punishment of the Hereafter light.
B) It increases one's wealth.
C) It makes one popular.
D) It is not beneficial.

Key Takeaway
Self-accounting is a daily discipline that prepares the believer for the final reckoning and lightens its burden.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you review your day tonight, will you honestly face the sins you committed, or will you rationalize them away? What would change if you truly believed every word you just read?
?
Which actions do you do habitually without ever pausing to ask, 'What is Allah's ruling on this?' What fear keeps you from that pre-action check?
?
If you knew that you would be asked about every moment of this day tomorrow, how would you spend the next hour? Are you living as if the recording angels are real?

The Practice of Self-Accounting
Commentator explains the duty and method of self-accounting.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the method and importance of self-accounting both after actions and before undertaking them.

Core Idea
Self-accounting in this life lightens the reckoning in the next.

The commentator begins by describing the practice of self-accounting, a key spiritual discipline. This passage details how to account for one's deeds daily and even before acting, supported by prophetic guidance.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on self-accounting
Read the Arabic passage once to get the commentator's flow. Then read the translation.
Notice the two levels of accounting: after action and before action.
2
Identify the two methods
Find where the commentator describes accounting after the day/night and accounting before every action.
3
Reflect on the hadith
Consider the hadith 'Account for yourselves before you are accounted for.' How does it reinforce the commentator's point?
4
Understand the benefit
The commentator says self-accounting in this world eases the punishment of the Hereafter. Think about why this is so.

[محاسبة النفس]
۸۷ - فَحاسِبِ النَّفْسَ وَقَلّل (۱) الامَلَا فَرُبَّ مَنْ جَدَّ لأَمْرٍ وَصَلَا قوله: (فَحاسِبِ النَّفْسَ) أي: إذا علمت أن عليك من يحفظ أعمالك ويكتبها فحاسب نفسك كل صباح على جميع ما عملته ليلاً، وكل مساء على جميع ما عملته نهاراً، فما وجدت من حسنةٍ حمدت الله عليها، أو من سيئة استغفرت الله منها، وأقرب من ذلك إلى السلامة أن تحاسبها على كل فعل قبل الإقدام عليه حتى لا تتلبس به إلا بعد معرفة حُكْم الله فيه، فما كان خيراً فعلته وما كان غير ذلك أمسكت عنه التعب؛ ولأن من حاسب نفسه في الدنيا هان عليه عذاب الآخرة، [الملائكة من [لتريح؟] وفي الحديث حاسبوا أنفسكم قبل أن تحاسبوا (2)
وقوله : ( وقَلَّلْ(۱) الَامَلَا) بفتح القاف وتشديد اللام الأولى وتسكين الثانية، ودرج همزة (الأملا) الثانية بنقل حركتها للامه؛ أي: قصر الأمل، وهو رجاء ما تحبُّه النفس كطول عُمْرٍ وزيادة غنى، وهو مذموم إلا من العلماء حيث أملوا طول عمرهم لنفع المسلمين، فيثابون على نياتهم في ذلك، والأصل فيما ذكر قوله : كُن في الدنيا كأنَّك غريب أو عابر سبيل، وعُدَّ نفسك من أهل القبور (2)، ومن كلام بعضهم : مَنْ قَصُرَ أملُهُ قَلَّ همُّه وتنور قلبه ورضي بالقليل، وبضدها تتميز الأشياء. وقوله : (فَرُبَّ مَنْ جَدَّ لأَمْرِ وَصَلَا) مرتبط بمحذوف يؤخذ من قوله: (وقلل(۳) الاملا)، والتقدير : وجُدَّ في مطلوبك فرُبَّ من جَدَّ لأمر وصلا ... إلى آخره؛ أي: لأنه رُبَّ من اجتهد بتوفيق الله له لتحصيل أمرٍ من أمور الدنيا أو الآخرة، وصل إلى ذلك لتقدير الله في الأزل وصوله إليه .
[Self-Accounting]. 87 - So account for the soul and shorten hopes, for perhaps one who strives for a matter reaches it. He said: (So account for the soul) meaning: when you know that there is over you one who preserves your deeds and writes them, then account for your soul every morning for all that you did at night, and every evening for all that you did during the day. Whatever good you find, praise Allah for it, or whatever evil, seek forgiveness from Allah for it. And closer than that to safety is to account for it before every action before undertaking it so that you do not engage in it except after knowing Allah's ruling concerning it. So whatever is good, do it, and whatever is not, refrain from it. And because whoever accounts for his soul in this world, the punishment of the Hereafter becomes light for him. [The angels?] [for ease?] And in the hadith: 'Account for yourselves before you are accounted for.' (2). And His saying: (And shorten the hope) with fath of the qaf and tashdid of the first lam and sukun of the second, and dropping the hamza of the second (al-amala) by transferring its haraka to the lam before it; meaning: shorten hope, which is hoping for what the soul loves like long life and increase of wealth. It is blameworthy except from the scholars, for they hope for long life to benefit the Muslims, so they are rewarded for their intentions in that. The basis for what was mentioned is His saying: 'Be in this world as if you were a stranger or a wayfarer, and count yourself among the people of the graves.' (2). And from the sayings of some of them: 'Whose hope is short, his worry decreases, his heart is illumined, and he is content with little. And by its opposite are things distinguished.' And His saying: (For perhaps one who strives for a matter reaches it) is connected to a deleted element taken from His saying: (And shorten hope). The estimation: And strive in your request, for perhaps one who strives for a matter reaches it ... etc.; meaning: for perhaps one who strives, with Allah's assistance, to obtain a matter of this world or the Hereafter, reaches it due to Allah's predetermination in eternity that he would reach it.
Source pages p. 323

Commentary
This passage provides a practical exhortation for spiritual self-accounting. It explains verse 87 of the poem. The commentator begins by describing the method of self-accounting: one should review their deeds each morning and evening, praising Allah for good deeds and seeking forgiveness for bad. He then recommends a more advanced method: accounting before acting, to ensure every action aligns with Allah's ruling. He cites the hadith urging self-accounting before the Day of Judgment. Then he explains the phrase 'وقَلّل الأمل' (shorten hope), clarifying its linguistic features and meaning: hope here refers to worldly desires like long life and wealth, which is blameworthy except for scholars who hope for long life to benefit others. He supports this with the prophetic advice to be like a stranger in this world and count oneself among the dead. He then quotes a wise saying: short hope leads to less worry, an illumined heart, and contentment. Finally, he explains the second half of the verse: he who strives with sincerity (with Allah's help) will reach his goal, as Allah has predetermined the outcome.

Key Terms
محاسبة النفس

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Self-accounting is only required after actions, not before.
Correction: The commentator emphasizes that accounting before every action is 'closer to safety' and prevents engaging in sin.

Checkpoint
According to the commentator, what is the benefit of self-accounting in this world?
A) It increases one's wealth
B) It makes the punishment of the Hereafter light
C) It guarantees entry into Paradise
D) It removes all sins

Key Takeaway
Self-accounting after and before actions is a vital practice that eases the accountability of the Hereafter.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When was the last time you reviewed your deeds before sleeping? What held you back from doing it tonight?
?
Are you more concerned with how others see your actions or with how Allah sees them? How would your day change if you accounted for every action before doing it?
?
If you believed that your self-accounting today directly reduces your punishment in the grave, would you still neglect it? What is stopping you right now?

Shortening Hopes and Striving
Commentator explains shortening hopes and the role of striving.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the virtue of shortening worldly hopes, the exception for scholars, and the role of striving with divine predetermination.

Core Idea
Short hopes bring peace of heart, but scholars may hope for long life to benefit others.

The commentator now explains the second part of the verse: shortening hopes and striving. He clarifies the meaning of 'hope', its blameworthiness, and the exception for scholars. Then he connects striving to reaching one's goal through Allah's predetermination.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on shortening hopes
Read the Arabic and translation carefully. Note the linguistic note about the word 'الأمل'.
The commentator gives a detailed grammatical explanation.
2
Understand the ruling on hope
Find where the commentator says hope is blameworthy and where he mentions the exception for scholars.
Think about why scholars are exempt.
3
Reflect on the prophetic advice
Consider the hadith 'Be in this world as a stranger' and the wise saying about short hopes.
4
Analyze the striving part
Read the final explanation: striving with Allah's help leads to success due to eternal predetermination.
Notice the connection between effort and divine decree.

?

?

?

Iktisab vs. Tawakkul: The Superiority Debate
The author presents two scholarly positions on the superiority of iktisab and tawakkul.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the scholarly disagreement over whether acquisition (iktisab) or trust in God (tawakkul) is superior, and the reasoning behind each position.

Core Idea
Both effort and trust are valid; the debate is about which is better, not which is right.

Is it better to actively seek provision through work and trade, or to rely entirely on God without engaging means? Classical scholars debated this question, each side offering compelling spiritual and practical arguments. This passage presents both views and hints that the answer depends on one's circumstances and spiritual state.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic poem line and the commentary, then the English translation. Notice that the author first states there is a disagreement, then defines each concept with its supporting arguments.
The phrase 'the correct view is the detail' suggests no absolute superiority.
2
Understand the definition of iktisab
Iktisab is engaging in permissible means like trade, medicine, etc. by choice. Its supporters argue it restrains the soul from depending on others and enables one to help others.
Note that iktisab is not blameworthy; it is a valid path.
3
Understand the definition of tawakkul
Tawakkul is reliance on God while being capable of using means, but choosing to disregard them. Its supporters argue it frees one from distraction and protects from the trials of wealth.
Tawakkul does not mean neglecting capability; it means not relying on means.
4
Reflect on the disagreement
The disagreement is about superiority (afdaliyya), not validity. Both sides have sound arguments. The author implies the answer is detailed, depending on the person and situation.
Consider a person's spiritual state: one may need to practice tawakkul to cure attachment, another may need iktisab to fulfill responsibilities.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فِي الاكْتِسابِ والتَّوَكُلِ اخْتُلِفْ والرَّاجِعُ التَّفْصِيْلُ حَسْبَمَا عُرِفْ
قوله : (فِي الاِكْتِسابِ والتَّوَكُلِ اخْتُلِفْ :أي: في أفضلية الاكتساب وأفضلية التوكل اختلف العلماء، فالخلافُ إنما هو في الأفضلية، فرجّح قوم الاكتساب ؛ وهو مباشرة الأسباب بالاختيار كالبيع والشراء لأجل الربح، ومثله تعاطي الدواء لأجل الصحة، ونحو ذلك ؛ وإنما رجحوه لما فيه من كفّ النفس عن التطلع لما في أيدي الناس، ومنعها من الخضوع لهم والتذلل بين أيديهم مع حيازة منصب التوسعة على عباد الله ومواساة المحتاجين وصلة الأرحام بتوفيق الله تعالى.
ورجح قومٌ التوكل ؛ وهو الاعتماد عليه تعالى، وقطع النظر عن الأسباب مع التمكن منها، وإنما رجحوه لما فيه من ترك ما يشغل عن الله تعالى، والاتصاف بالرغبة إلى الله تعالى والوثوق بما عنده مع حيازة مقام السلامة من فتنة المال والمحاسبة عليه .
On acquisition (al-iktisab) and trust (al-tawakkul) there is disagreement, and the correct view is the detail according to what is known.
His statement: 'On acquisition and trust there is disagreement' means: concerning the superiority of acquisition and the superiority of trust, the scholars disagreed. The dispute is only about superiority. So some people preferred acquisition, which is the direct engagement of means by choice, like buying and selling for profit, and likewise taking medicine for health, and the like. They preferred it because it restrains the soul from looking at what people possess, prevents it from abasing itself before them, while obtaining the position of providing ease for God's servants, helping the needy, and maintaining family ties by God's success.
And some people preferred trust, which is reliance on Him and disregarding means while still being capable of them. They preferred it because it involves leaving what distracts from God, being characterized by desire for God and confidence in what He possesses, while obtaining the station of safety from the trial of wealth and accountability for it.
Source pages p. 388

Commentary
This passage discusses the scholarly disagreement about whether iktisab (acquisition through means) or tawakkul (trust in God) is superior. The author clarifies that the disagreement concerns preference, not validity. He defines iktisab as directly engaging means by choice, such as trade or medicine. Those who prefer it argue that it restrains the soul from coveting others' possessions, prevents humiliation, and enables one to benefit others. He defines tawakkul as reliance on God while being capable of using means, but disregarding them. Those who prefer it argue that it frees one from distraction, fosters reliance on God, and protects from the trials of wealth. The author does not take sides but implies that the correct view is a detailed one depending on circumstances, as hinted by the poem line 'the correct view is the detail according to what is known'.

Key Terms
اكتساب (iktisab)
توكل (tawakkul)
أفضلية (superiority)
أسباب (means)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One must choose either complete reliance with no effort or complete effort without trust.
Correction: Both iktisab and tawakkul are valid; the debate is about which is superior in specific contexts, not that one excludes the other.

Checkpoint
What is the main point of disagreement regarding iktisab and tawakkul according to this passage?
A) Whether iktisab is permissible at all.
B) Whether tawakkul is obligatory.
C) Which of the two is superior.
D) Both are forbidden without proper intention.

Key Takeaway
The scholarly debate on iktisab vs. tawakkul is about superiority, not validity; both paths have spiritual merits, and the best choice depends on one's circumstances and spiritual state.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you face a difficulty, do your actions reveal that you truly believe the iktisab group's reasoning (fear of neediness) or the tawakkul group's (desire to focus on God alone)? What would change if you prioritized spiritual superiority over personal comfort?
?
Which of the two spiritual dangers—humiliation from people or distraction from God—do you feel is more relevant to your current life stage? Write down one concrete step you can take today to guard against that danger, based on the scholars' arguments in this lesson.
?
The author says the correct view is 'detail according to what is known.' If you had to give your own detailed verdict for your own life right now, would you lean more toward iktisab or tawakkul? Why? Are you willing to accept that your preference might change with circumstances, and that both are valid paths to God?

Defining al-Mawjud and Refuting the Sophists
Definition of al-mawjud and refutation of the Sophists

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that al-mawjud is defined as that which is fixed in the external world and how this definition serves to refute the Sophists who deny objective reality.

Core Idea
The existent is not an illusion; it is real and observable.

In this passage, the commentator unpacks a line from the poem that defines 'the existent' (al-mawjud) as that which is fixed in the external world. This definition is not merely grammatical; it serves a crucial polemical purpose: to refute the Sophists, who deny objective reality. Through an anecdote about Imam Abu Hanifa, we see the practical absurdity of their position.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the literal translation. Notice the grammatical structure and the story at the end.
The passage combines a definition with a refutation.
2
Understand the grammatical structure
The phrase 'And fixed in the external is the existent' is a nominal sentence with a fronted predicate. This emphasizes external existence as the defining feature.
3
Identify the purpose
The commentator states that the purpose is to refute the Sophists, who deny the realities of things.
Al-'Aqa'id al-Nasafiyya is cited in support.
4
Reflect on the anecdote
The story of Imam Abu Hanifa and the Sophist shows the practical inconsistency of sophism: if nothing is real, why seek your mule?
5
Summarize the key point
Conclude that al-mawjud means external, observable reality, and that this definition counters skepticism about the external world.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقوله : وثَابِتُ في الخَارِج المَوْجُودُ جملة من مبتدإ وخبر، فـ (ثابت في الخارج) خبر مقدَّم، و(الموجود) مبتدأ مؤخّر، يعني أن الثابت في الخارج بحيث تصح رؤيته هو الموجود (۱) ، وغرضه بذلك الردُّ على السوفسطائية الذين ينكرون حقائق الأشياء، ويزعمون أنها خيالات، ولذلك قال في أوّل العقائد (۲) : حقائق الأشياء ثابتة، والعلم بها متحقق، خلافاً للسوفسطائية). وقد حُكي أن سوفسطائيًا أتى على بغلة إلى الإمام أبي حنيفة ليناظره، فأمر الإمام بعض تلامذته أن يذهب بالبغلة، فلما خرج السوفسطائي لم يجدها فطلبها، فقال له الإمام أنت تزعم أنه لم يكن لبغلتك حقيقة فلا تطلبها فرجع عن معتقده، ورُدَّت إليه بغلته .
And his saying: 'And fixed in the external is the existent' is a sentence composed of a subject and predicate. 'Fixed in the external' is an advanced predicate, and 'the existent' is a delayed subject. It means that that which is fixed in the external such that its vision is correct is the existent. And his purpose by that is the refutation of the Sophists, who deny the realities of things and claim that they are illusions. Therefore he said in the beginning of al-'Aqa'id: 'The realities of things are fixed, and knowledge of them is realized, contrary to the Sophists.' And it has been narrated that a Sophist came on a mule to Imam Abu Hanifa to debate him. The Imam ordered some of his students to take the mule. When the Sophist came out and did not find it, he asked for it. The Imam said to him: 'You claim that your mule had no reality, so do not seek it.' So he recanted his belief, and his mule was returned to him.
Source pages p. 391

Commentary
This passage is a definition combined with a refutation. The commentator explains the line of the poem that reads 'And fixed in the external is the existent' (وثابت في الخارج الموجود). He analyzes it grammatically as a nominal sentence with a fronted predicate ('fixed in the external') and a delayed subject ('the existent'). The meaning is that the existent (الموجود) is that which is established in the external world such that it can be seen. The commentator then states the purpose of this definition: to refute the Sophists (السوفسطائية), who deny objective realities and claim that things are mere illusions. He supports this by citing al-'Aqa'id al-Nasafiyya, which affirms that the realities of things are fixed and knowable. To illustrate the refutation, he recounts an anecdote: a Sophist came to debate Imam Abu Hanifa on a mule. The Imam secretly had the mule taken away. When the Sophist could not find it and asked for it, the Imam pointed out the inconsistency—if the Sophist truly believed the mule had no reality, he should not seek it. The Sophist was convinced and recanted, and the mule was returned. This anecdote demonstrates the practical absurdity of sophism.

Key Terms
الموجود
السوفسطائية
ثابت في الخارج

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that 'موجود' includes mental existence or illusions.
Correction: The passage specifies that al-mawjud is what is fixed in the external world and can be seen, thus excluding mental or illusory existence.

Checkpoint
Why does the commentator define 'al-mawjud' as 'fixed in the external'?
A) To establish a grammatical rule for the poem
B) To refute the Sophists who deny objective reality
C) To argue that existence is only mental
D) To prove the existence of God

Key Takeaway
Al-mawjud is defined as external reality knowable by sight, and this definition refutes any denial of objective reality.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever acted as if something is real while claiming it does not matter or is just a 'social construct'? Where in your life are your actions belying your words, and what reality are you trying to escape by denying it?
?
The Sophist sought his mule even after denying its reality. What is the 'mule' in your own life—something you rely on daily but hesitate to acknowledge as objectively true or important? Are you willing to let the Imam's question strip away your pretense?
?
If you truly believed that nothing is real, you would not seek anything. Yet you seek comfort, meaning, and truth. Why do you keep searching? What does that search say about the reality you claim to doubt?

The Proof for Obligation of Repentance: Revelation or Reason?
The commentator contrasts the Ash'ari claim of revelatory proof with the Mu'tazili claim of rational proof for repentance's obligation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the difference between the Ash'ari and Mu'tazili proofs for the obligation of repentance.

Core Idea
Repentance is obligatory by consensus, but its proof divides Ash'aris and Mu'tazila.

This passage focuses on a key methodological disagreement between the Ash'aris and Mu'tazila. While both agree that repentance is individually obligatory, they differ on the source of this obligation. The passage contrasts the Ash'ari reliance on revelation with the Mu'tazili reliance on reason, highlighting a foundational difference in Islamic theology.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic and translation
Start by reading the Arabic text and its translation to get the literal meaning. Notice the key terms: sam'i and aqli.
The passage is short but dense.
2
Identify the two opposing positions
Find where the Ash'ari position is stated and where the Mu'tazili position is stated. What evidence does each side use?
3
Understand the Mu'tazili principle
The passage mentions the Mu'tazili doctrine of rational approbation and disapprobation (tahsin wa taqbih aqliyyan). Why does the commentator call it corrupt?
4
Reflect on the sectarian difference
Consider how this disagreement reflects a broader methodological divide between Ash'aris and Mu'tazila. How does each approach affect other theological rulings?
This is a classic point of contention.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولا خلاف في وجوب التوبة عيناً ؛ وإنما الخلاف في دليل الوجوب، فعندنا دليله سمعي، كقوله تعالى: ﴿وَتُوبُوا إِلَى اللهِ جَمِيعًا أَيُّهَ الْمُؤْمِنُونَ ﴾ [النور: ۳۱)، وعند المعتزلة دليله عقليّ ؛ لأنَّ العقل يدرك حسنها، وما أدرك العقلُ حسنه فهو واجب بناءً على مذهبهم الفاسد من أن الأحكام تابعة للتحسين والتقبيح العقليين.
And there is no disagreement regarding the individual obligation of repentance; rather, the disagreement is over the proof of obligation. According to us, its proof is revelational (samʿī), such as His statement, Exalted: 'And repent to Allah, all of you, O believers' (Quran 24:31). According to the Muʿtazila, its proof is rational (ʿaqlī), because the intellect perceives its goodness, and whatever the intellect perceives as good is obligatory, based on their corrupt doctrine that rulings follow rational approbation and disapprobation (al-taḥsīn wa al-taqbīḥ al-ʿaqliyān).
Source pages p. 396

Commentary
This passage addresses the proof (dalīl) for the obligation of repentance. There is consensus that repentance is individually obligatory (wājib ʿaynī), but the disagreement concerns the source of this obligation. The Ashʿarīs (and the commentator's school) hold that the proof is from revelation (samʿī), citing the Quranic verse. The Muʿtazila, on the other hand, argue that reason alone suffices: the intellect recognizes the goodness of repentance, and according to their principle, whatever the intellect deems good is obligatory. The commentator dismisses this as a corrupt doctrine, referring to the Muʿtazilī theory of rational good and evil (al-taḥsīn wa al-taqbīḥ al-ʿaqliyān).

Key Terms
سمعي
عقلي
تحسين وتقبيح عقليين
دليل الوجوب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that repentance's obligation is disputed among Muslims.
Correction: The passage clarifies that there is consensus (no disagreement) that repentance is individually obligatory; the dispute is only over the proof (evidence) for that obligation, not the obligation itself.

Checkpoint
According to the Ash'aris, what is the source of the obligation of repentance?
A) Rational proof
B) Revelational proof
C) Both
D) Consensus

Key Takeaway
The obligation of repentance is agreed upon, but the proof differs: Ash'aris rely on revelation, Mu'tazila on reason.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you repent, is your motivation rooted in the fear of Allah's command (as in the verse) or in your own rational conviction that repentance is good? Which of these two motivations is stronger in your life right now, and what does that reveal about your faith?
?
If you were to rely solely on your intellect to decide when to repent, how often would you actually repent? Think of a recent sin you committed—did you immediately repent because you felt the weight of Allah's command, or did you rationalize it away? What would change if you made the verse your immediate trigger for repentance?
?
The Ash'aris insist that repentance is obligatory by revelation, not reason. Does your daily practice reflect that you see repentance as a direct command from Allah that must be obeyed without delay, or do you treat it as a casual recommendation that you can postpone? What one change can you make today to treat repentance with the urgency it deserves?

The Obligation of Repentance: Scriptural vs Rational Proof
Author asserts obligation of repentance, contrasts scriptural vs intellectual proof.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Ash'ari position that the proof for the obligation of repentance is scriptural, not rational, and the contrast with the Mu'tazila view.

Core Idea
The proof for repentance is from revelation, not reason alone.

Repentance is an obligation all Muslims agree on, but how do we know it is obligatory? This lesson presents the Ash'ari answer: the evidence comes from the Quran, not from independent reason. We will also see how the Mu'tazila differed and why the Ash'aris call their view corrupt.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing it. Then read the translation. Notice the structure: consensus first, then two positions.
The author uses a concise, comparative style.
2
Identify the two proofs
Find 'سمعي' (scriptural) for Ash'aris and 'عقلي' (intellectual) for Mu'tazila. Note the Quranic verse cited as scriptural evidence.
Verse 24:31 is used as proof.
3
Understand the refutation
The author dismisses the Mu'tazila position as 'فاسد' (corrupt) due to their doctrine of 'تحسين وتقبيح عقليين' (intellectual approval/disapproval). Reflect on why this matters.
This highlights a key difference in theological methodology.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولا خلاف في وجوب التوبة عيناً؛ وإنما الخلاف في دليل الوجوب، فعندنا دليله سمعي، كقوله تعالى: ﴿وَتُوبُوا إِلَى اللهِ جَمِيعًا أَيُّهَ الْمُؤْمِنُونَ﴾، وعند المعتزلة دليله عقليّ؛ لأنَّ العقل يدرك حسنها، وما أدرك العقلُ حسنه فهو واجب بناءً على مذهبهم الفاسد من أن الأحكام تابعة للتحسين والتقبيح العقليين.
And there is no disagreement regarding the individual obligation of repentance; rather the disagreement is concerning the proof of the obligation. According to us, its proof is scriptural, such as His saying: 'And turn in repentance to Allah all together, O believers.' According to the Mu'tazila, its proof is intellectual, because the intellect perceives its goodness, and whatever the intellect perceives as good is obligatory, based on their corrupt doctrine that rulings follow intellectual approval and disapproval.
Source pages p. 396

Commentary
This passage addresses the obligation of repentance. The author states that there is consensus on it being an individual obligation, but the proof differs: the Ash'aris rely on scriptural evidence (sam'i) like the Quranic verse, while the Mu'tazila rely on rational evidence (aqli), arguing that the intellect alone can determine the goodness of repentance and thus its obligation. The Five Universal Necessities: Definition and Objection
Definition of the five/six universal necessities and response to an objection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the concept of the five (or six) universal necessities and how the objection regarding the permissibility of alcohol in early Islam is answered.

Core Idea
The five/six universal necessities are never fully permitted in any religious community.

The commentator introduces the famous doctrine of al-kulliyyat al-khams, the five universal necessities that Islamic law aims to protect: religion, life, property, lineage, and intellect, with honor sometimes added. He then addresses a potential objection regarding the permissibility of alcohol in early Islam. This lesson unpacks the definition and the objection-answer.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the literal translation. Notice the poetic line and the commentary.
The passage is clear but dense; take your time.
2
Identify the five/six items
List the items mentioned: religion, life, property, lineage, intellect, and honor. Note that some scholars count five by subsuming honor under lineage.
The text mentions both options.
3
Understand the objection and answer
Consider the objection about alcohol being initially permitted. Then see how the commentator answers: either the totality was never permitted, or it refers to the final settled state of our law.
This shows Islamic legal theory handling apparent contradictions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
١٢٧ - وَحِفْظُ دِيْنِ ثُمَّ نَفْسٍ مَالَ نَسَبْ ومِثْلُها عَقْل وَعِرْضُ قَدْ وَجَبْ
قوله : (وَحِفْظُ ... إلخ) هذا شروع في المسألة المعروفة عند القوم بالكليات الخمس أو الست وهو الموافق للمتن حيث جعل العِرْض مستقلا عن النسب، فمن جعل العِرْض راجعاً للنسب عبّر عنها بالكليات الخمس، ومن جعله مُستقِلا عن عبر عنها بالكليات الست، وإنما سميت بالكليات؛ لأنه يتفرع عليها أحكام كثيرة ؛ ولأنها وجبت في كل مِلَّةٍ فلم تُبَحْ في مِلَّة من الملل .
فإن قيل : يرد عليه أن شرب الخمر كان جائزاً في صدر الإسلام بوحي، وتكرر النسخ له .
أجيب بأن المراد أن المجموع لم يُبح في مِلَّةٍ من الملل، أو أنه باعتبار ما استقر عليه أمر ملتِنا .
127 - 'And preserving religion, then life, property, lineage, and similar to them intellect and honor has become obligatory.' His saying: ('And preserving... etc.') This is the beginning of the issue known among the people as the five or six universal [necessities]. And it is in agreement with the matn where it makes honor independent of lineage. So whoever makes honor refer back to lineage expresses them as the five universals, and whoever makes it independent of it expresses them as the six universals. And they are called universal only because many rulings branch from them, and because they are obligatory in every religious community, so they were not permitted in any community. An objection is raised: that drinking alcohol was permitted in early Islam by revelation, and its abrogation occurred repeatedly. The answer is that the intended meaning is that the totality was not permitted in any religious community, or that it is according to what the matter of our community has settled upon.
Source pages p. 397

Commentary
This passage is the introduction to the section on the five (or six) universal necessities (al-kulliyyat al-khams aw al-sitt). It identifies the subject of the poem line: the obligation to preserve five/six things: religion, life, property, lineage, intellect, and honor (the sixth, which some subsume under lineage). The commentator explains why they are called 'universal': many rulings depend on them, and they are obligatory in every revealed law (milla), never completely permitted. Then he addresses an objection: alcohol (khamr) was initially allowed in Islam through revelation, then abrogated, which seems to violate the claim that these were never permitted. The answer: either the 'totality' (al-majmūʿ) was never permitted in any community (i.e., not all of them together), or the statement is according to the final settled state of our law (i.e., after abrogation, alcohol became forbidden and remained so).

Key Terms
كليات
دين
نفس
مال
نسب
عقل
عرض
ملة
نسخ

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The claim that the five necessities were never permitted in any community is disproved by the fact that alcohol was initially allowed in Islam.
Correction: The text clarifies that the 'totality' was never permitted, or the statement refers to the final settled state of Islamic law after abrogation.

Checkpoint
Why are the five necessities called 'universal' (kulliyyat) according to the text?
A) Because they apply to all Muslims
B) Because many rulings branch from them and they are obligatory in every religious community
C) Because they are the foundations of all Islamic law
D) Because they are derived from the Quran and Sunnah

Key Takeaway
The five (or six) universal necessities are foundational protections in Islamic law that are never fully permitted in any religious community, and historical exceptions are resolved by considering the totality or the final state of the law.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
How have you compromised on protecting your intellect by consuming content that dulls your mind or by engaging in activities that impair your reasoning? What concrete step will you take today to restore that protection?
?
Of the six universal necessities—religion, life, property, lineage, intellect, and honor—which one do you most neglect in your daily choices? What specific change will you make starting this week to actively preserve that necessity?
?
If preserving religion is an absolute necessity in every community, what have you done in the past 24 hours to strengthen your own faith? What obstacle kept you from doing more, and how will you remove it tomorrow?

The Order of the Five Universal Necessities
Enumeration and ranking of the five necessities with scholarly debate.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the priority order of religion, life, lineage, intellect, property, and honor, and the rationales for this ordering.

Core Idea
The order of necessities reflects their relative importance in preserving religion and society.

The commentator discusses the priority among the universal necessities (al-kulliyyāt al-khams). The order is not arbitrary but based on how essential each is for upholding the faith and worldly life. Disagreements among scholars are noted and evaluated based on textual evidence and rational comparison of penalties.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage on order
Read the first part of the passage which lists the order and the reasons for each priority. Notice the comparisons between lineage and intellect, and between property and honor.
Pay attention to the phrase 'والأول أولى'.
2
Understand the reasoning for religion first
Religion is given top priority because preserving everything else is a means to preserve religion. Consider the central role of faith in Islam.
3
Analyze the debate on lineage vs intellect
The passage presents both views: some put lineage before intellect, some reverse. The commentator supports lineage first because adultery is more severely prohibited than wine.
This shows how fiqh rulings inform the ordering.
4
Examine the ranking of property and honor
Property and honor are last. There is a sub-debate: honor may be equal to property unless it involves lineage. Sanusi argues for property's precedence due to harsher penalties.
Note the mention of theft and highway robbery versus slander.
5
Reflect on the overall principle
Think about why the ordering matters for legal theory and priority-setting in Islamic law.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأكد هذه الأمور الدين؛ لأن حفظ غيره وسيلة لحفظه، ثم النفس؛ لأن قتل النفس يلي الكفر، كما تقدم، ثم النسب، ثم العقل، وبعضهم قدم العقل على النسب، والأول أولى؛ لأن الزنا أشدُّ تحريماً من شرب الخمر، ثم المال، وفي مرتبته العرض، إن لم يؤد الطعن فيه إلى قطع نسب ؛ فإن أدى إلى ذلك؛ كأن قذف زوجته بالزنا، ونفى ولدها عنه، فهو في مرتبة النسب، ومنهم من يقدِّمُ العِرْض على المال. قال السنوسي : والذي يظهر لو قيل به عكسه؛ لأن العقوبة المترتبة على أخذ الأموال، كما في السرقة وقطع الطريق .. أعظمُ من العقوبة المترتبة على الخوض في الأعراض، كما في القذف.
وقوله : (دِيْن) أي: ما شرعه الله تعالى لعباده من الأحكام، والمراد بحفظه صيانته عن الكفر وانتهاك حرمة المحرمات ووجوب الواجبات؛ فانتهاك حرمة المحرمات أن يفعل المحرمات غير مبال بحرمتها ؛ وانتهاك وجوب الواجبات أن يترك الواجبات غير مبال بوجوبها ولحفظ الدين شرع قتال الكفار الحربيين وغيرهم كالمرتدين
وقوله : (ثُمَّ نَفْسٍ ) أي : عاقلة، ولو بحسب الشأن، فيدخل الصغير والمجنون، وتخرج البهيمة، فيتصرف الشخص فيها بالوجه الشرعي؛ كالذبح وغيره إن كانت له، فإن كانت لغيره فهي داخلة في المال ؛ ولحفظ النفس شرع القصاص في النفس والطرف؛ لأنه ربما أدى إلى النفس.
And the most emphasized of these matters is religion, because preserving others is a means to preserving it. Then life, because killing life comes after disbelief, as previously mentioned. Then lineage, then intellect. And some place intellect before lineage, but the first is more appropriate because adultery is more severely prohibited than drinking wine. Then property, and in its rank is honor, if impugning it does not lead to cutting off lineage. If it does lead to that, such as accusing one's wife of adultery and denying her child from him, then it is in the rank of lineage. And among them are those who place honor before property. Sanusi said: 'What appears is that if one were to say the opposite, it is because the punishment entailed for taking property, as in theft and highway robbery, is greater than the punishment entailed for attacking honor, as in slander.' His saying: (religion) meaning: what Allah the Exalted has legislated for His servants of rulings. And the intent of preserving it is safeguarding it from disbelief and violating the sanctity of prohibitions and the obligation of obligations. Violating the sanctity of prohibitions is to do forbidden acts without caring for their sanctity; violating the obligation of obligations is to leave obligatory acts without caring for their obligation. And for the preservation of religion, fighting against warring disbelievers and others like apostates is legislated. His saying: (then life) meaning: rational life, even if potentially, so it includes the child and the insane, and excludes animals. So a person may dispose of it in a legal manner, like slaughter and other things if it belongs to him. If it belongs to another, then it falls under property. And for preserving life, retaliation (qisas) for life and limb is legislated, because it may lead to loss of life.
Source pages p. 398

Commentary
This passage explains the ordering of the universal necessities and defines the first two. It begins by stating that religion is the most emphasized because preserving the other necessities is a means to preserve religion (i.e., religious life). Next is life, because killing life is a major sin second only to disbelief. Then lineage, then intellect; some reverse the order, but the commentator argues lineage before intellect because adultery (zina) which violates lineage is more severely forbidden than wine which affects intellect. Then property, with honor (ʿirḍ) at the same rank unless attacking honor implicates lineage (e.g., false accusation of adultery against a spouse denies lineage), in which case honor ranks with lineage. Some place honor above property, but Sanusi argues that the opposite is more apparent because the penalty for property crimes (theft, highway robbery) is more severe than for slander. Then the passage defines 'dīn' as the law itself; preserving it means guarding against disbelief and showing disregard for prohibitions and obligations, and fighting disbelievers and apostates is prescribed for its preservation. Then 'nafs' is defined as a rational soul, including children and insane; it can be disposed of lawfully, and if it belongs to another it is property; qisās is legislated to preserve life.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think lineage and intellect are equally important or that intellect should always come first.
Correction: The text argues lineage takes precedence because adultery (violating lineage) is more severely prohibited than drinking wine (violating intellect). This is based on the severity of sin, not inherent value.

Checkpoint
According to the text, why is lineage placed before intellect in the order of the universal necessities?
A) Because lineage is more important for social order
B) Because the punishment for adultery is more severe than for drinking wine
C) Because intellect is already preserved by lineage
D) Because intellect is not a necessity

Key Takeaway
The order of the five necessities reflects their relative importance in Islamic law, with religion first, then life, lineage, intellect, property, and honor, supported by textual and rational arguments.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Look at the ranking: religion first, then life, then lineage, then intellect, then property. Where does your own daily effort and concern actually lie? If your heart's true priority were exposed, would it match this divine order? What one concrete change can you make today to realign your priorities?
?
The passage teaches that attacking lineage through adultery is more severe than attacking intellect through wine. Do you treat sins against family honor and sexual morality as gravely as you treat sins like drinking? Or have you become desensitized to the severity of zina? Think of your own attitude—what does it reveal about your internalization of this ranking?
?
When you are slandered or your honor is attacked, do you demand justice and feel your status diminished? Yet property crimes carry heavier punishments. Are you more protective of your wealth than your reputation? If so, why? Let this lesson search your heart: do you value material loss over personal dignity in a way that contradicts the Islamic order?

Defining Religion and Life and Their Preservation
Definitions of religion and life and the legal means to preserve them.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definitions of religion (dīn) and life (nafs) in the context of the universal necessities, and how their preservation is legislated.

Core Idea
Preserving religion means guarding against disbelief and disregard for obligations; preserving life includes retaliation for intentional killing.

After establishing the priority order, the commentator defines the first two necessities: religion and life. These definitions clarify what it means to 'preserve' them and illustrate the legal measures taken to protect them—fighting disbelievers for religion, and qisas for life.

Guided Path
1
Read the definitions of religion
Read the passage defining 'dīn' as the legislated rulings. Understand that preserving it involves avoiding disbelief and taking obligations seriously.
Note the two aspects: guarding against violation of prohibitions and neglect of obligations.
2
Read the definition of life (nafs)
The passage defines 'nafs' as rational life, including children and insane. Understand the scope: it excludes animals, and if it belongs to another, it becomes property.
3
Learn the preservation mechanisms
For religion, fighting disbelievers and apostates is prescribed. For life, qisas (retaliation) is legislated for life and limb.
The rationale for qisas is that it deters killing.
4
Reflect on the comprehensiveness
Notice how preserving each necessity involves both preventive and punitive measures, tailored to the nature of the necessity.

Evidence for the Obligation of Commanding Good and Forbidding Evil
Author cites consensus and scriptural proofs for the obligation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the foundational evidence from consensus, the Quran, and hadith that establishes commanding good and forbidding evil as an obligatory duty.

Core Idea
Commanding good and forbidding evil is a duty proven by consensus, Quran, and prophetic warning.

The obligation of commanding good and forbidding evil is not merely a recommendation but a duty that early Muslims practiced. In this lesson, we examine the key textual proofs that underpin this duty, including a clarifying verse and a stern hadith against rejecting admonition.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on evidence
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Note how the author introduces each piece of evidence.
The author starts with consensus, then addresses a potential objection from the Quran.
2
Identify the three proofs
List the three proofs: consensus, Quran 5:105, and the hadith. Understand how each supports the obligation.
The hadith warns against angrily rejecting advice.
3
Reflect on the hadith's lesson
Consider the severity of rejecting admonition. How does this reinforce the duty?

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما الإجماعُ فلأن المسلمين في الصدر الأول وبعده كانوا يتواصون بذلك، ويوبخون تاركه مع الاقتدار عليه ولا يشكل على وجوب الأمر بالمعروف والنهي عن المنكر قوله تعالى: ﴿يَأَيُّهَا الَّذِينَ آمَنُوا عَلَيْكُمْ أَنفُسَكُمْ لَا يَضُرُكُم مِّن ضَلَّ إِذَا اهْتَدَيْتُهُ﴾ [المائدة: ١٠٥]؛ لأن المعنى إذا فعلتم ما كُلّفتم به ؛ ومنه الأمر بالمعروف والنهي عن المنكر .. لا يضركم فعل غيركم للمعصية، فصارت الآية دالة على وجوب الأمر بالمعروف والنهي عن المنكر، قال ابن مسعود : إن من أكبر الذنوب عند الله أن يُقال للعبد : اتق الله فيقول : عليك بنفسك). وفي الحديث : «مَنْ قيل له : اتق الله، فغضب وقفَ يوم القيامة، فلم يبقَ ملك إلا مرَّ به وقال له : أنت الذي قيل لك : اتق الله ، فغضبت»؛ يعني يوبخونه. واعلم أن لوجوب الأمر بالمعروف والنهي عن المنكر شروطاً : أحدها: أن يكون المتولي لذلك عالماً بما يأمر به وينهى عنه، فالجاهل بالحكم لا يحل له الأمر ولا النهي فليس للعوام أمر ولا نهي فيما يجهلونه؛ وأما الذي استوى في معرفته العام والخاص ففيه للعالم وغيره الأمر بالمعروف والنهي عن المنكر . كأن ينهي عن شرب الخمر وثانيها : أن يأمن أن يُؤدي إنكاره إلى منكرٍ أكبر منه ، فيؤدي نهيه عنه إلى قتل النفس أو نحوه فعدم هذين الشرطين يوجب التحريم . وثالثها : أن يغلب على ظنه أن أمره بالمعروف مُؤكِّرٌ في تحصيله، وأن تهيه عن المنكر مزيل له، وعدم هذا الشرط يُسقط الوجوب، ويبقي الجواز إذا قطع بعدم الإفادة، والندب إذا شك فيها . قاله القرافي وغيره. وقال السعد والآمدي بالوجوب فيما لو ظنَّ عدم الإفادة، أو شك فيها، بخلاف ما إذا قطع بعدم الإفادة. ولفظ السعد ومن الشروط تجويز التأثير بألا يعلم قطعاً عدم التأثير، لئلا يكون عبثاً واشتغالاً بما لا يعني انتهى. ونحوه قول الآمدي : من شروط الوجوب ألا ييأس من إجابته، انتهى. وقال أكثرُ العلماء كالشافعية : لا يشترط هذا الشرط ؛ لأن الذي عليه الأمر والنهي لا القبول، كما قال تعالى: ﴿مَا عَلَى الرَّسُولِ إِلَّا الْبَلَاعُ﴾ [المائدة: ٩٩]، وقال تعالى: ﴿وَذَكِّرْ فَإِنَّ الذِّكْرَى تَنفَعُ الْمُؤْمِنِينَ﴾ [الذاريات: ٥٥]؛ ولذلك قال النووي: قال العلماء: ولا يسقط عن المكلف الأمر بالمعروف والنهي عن المنكر ؛ لكونه لا يفيد في ظنه، بل يجب عليه فعله. انتهى ملخصاً من «شرح المصنف»، ومن حاشية الشنواني».
As for consensus, the Muslims in the early period and after used to enjoin one another to that, and rebuke the one who abandons it while able. And it is not problematic for the obligation of commanding good and forbidding evil the saying of the Exalted: 'O you who have believed, upon yourselves is [responsibility]. Those who have gone astray will not harm you when you have been guided' (Quran 5:105); because the meaning is: when you do what you are charged with—including commanding good and forbidding evil—the sin of others does not harm you. So the verse indicates the obligation of commanding good and forbidding evil. Ibn Mas'ud said: 'Among the greatest sins with Allah is that it is said to a servant: Fear Allah, and he replies: Mind yourself.' And in the hadith: 'Whoever is told: Fear Allah, and becomes angry, will stop on the Day of Resurrection, and no angel will pass by him except he says to him: You are the one who was told: Fear Allah, and you became angry'—meaning they rebuke him. And know that the obligation of commanding good and forbidding evil has conditions: One of them: that the one undertaking that be knowledgeable of what he commands and forbids; for the ignorant of the ruling is not permitted to command or forbid, so the common people have no command or forbidding in what they are ignorant of. As for that which is equally known to common and specific, both the scholar and others may command good and forbid evil—like forbidding drinking wine. The second: that he be secure that his forbidding will not lead to a greater evil, such as his forbidding leading to killing of a soul or the like; the absence of these two conditions necessitates prohibition. The third: that he be predominantly certain that his commanding good will be effective in attaining it, and his forbidding evil will remove it; the absence of this condition removes obligation, and leaves permissibility if he is certain of lack of benefit, and recommendation if he doubts it. Al-Qarafi and others said this. Al-Sa'd and al-Amidi said obligation even if he thinks lack of benefit or doubts it, contrary to when he is certain of lack of benefit. Al-Sa'd's wording: 'Among the conditions is allowing for effect, such that he does not know for certain the lack of effect, to avoid futility and occupation with what does not concern him.' End quote. Similar is the saying of al-Amidi: 'Among the conditions of obligation is that he not despair of his response.' End quote. Most scholars, like the Shafi'is, said: this condition is not required, because the one who commands and forbids is only responsible for the command and prohibition, not acceptance, as the Exalted said: 'Not upon the Messenger is except conveyance' (Quran 5:99), and said: 'And remind, for indeed the reminder benefits the believers' (Quran 51:55). Therefore al-Nawawi said: The scholars said: It does not drop from the responsible person commanding good and forbidding evil due to his thinking it will not benefit; rather it is obligatory upon him. End quote, summarized from 'Sharh al-Musannaf' and from 'Hashiyat al-Shanwani'.
Source pages p. 406

Commentary
This passage discusses the basis and conditions for the obligation of commanding good and forbidding evil. The author begins by citing consensus (ijma') as evidence: early Muslims exhorted one another and rebuked those who neglected it. He then addresses a potential objection from Quran 5:105 ('Upon yourselves is your responsibility'), explaining that the verse means if you fulfill your duty—which includes commanding good and forbidding evil—the misguidance of others does not harm you, so the verse actually supports the obligation. He quotes a hadith warning against angrily rejecting admonition. The author then lists three conditions for obligation: (1) the person must have knowledge of what he commands and forbids; commoners may only act on matters equally known to all, like forbidding alcohol. (2) The forbidding must not lead to a greater evil (e.g., death); if it does, it becomes forbidden. (3) There must be a predominant expectation of efficacy; if one is certain of no effect, it becomes permissible (not obligatory), and if doubtful, recommended. The author notes a difference of opinion: al-Qarafi holds the condition of expected efficacy; al-Sa'd and al-Amidi hold that obligation remains even if one expects no effect, except when certain of no effect; but most scholars (e.g., Shafi'is) do not require this condition, citing verses that the duty is only to convey (Quran 5:99, 51:55), and al-Nawawi affirms that it remains obligatory regardless of apparent benefit.

Key Terms
الأمر بالمعروف
النهي عن المنكر
الإجماع

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the meaning of Quran 5:105 in relation to commanding good and forbidding evil?
A) It abrogates the duty.
B) It indicates that if you fulfill your duty, others' sins do not harm you.
C) It says to ignore others' actions.
D) It applies only to scholars.

Key Takeaway
The obligation of commanding good and forbidding evil is firmly established by consensus, the Quran, and prophetic warning, and rejecting admonition is a grave sin.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

?

?
On the Day when every angel rebukes those who rejected admonition, will you be among them? What step will you take today to ensure you listen when truth is spoken to you?
