Understanding the Command: Doing What is Permitted and Leaving What is Not
Commentary defining the scope of permitted and prohibited acts.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand what the verse's command to do what is permitted actually includes—namely, all actions not prohibited—and how it encompasses obligatory, recommended, and permissible actions, while the command to leave what is not permitted covers all prohibited actions.

Core Idea
The 'permitted' includes far more than just the optional—it covers everything not forbidden.

This lesson unpacks a brief but crucial commentary on a verse that commands us to do what is permitted and leave what is not. At first glance, the verse seems simple, but the commentator clarifies that 'what is permitted' actually refers to anything that is not prohibited, including obligatory and recommended acts. Understanding this definition helps us see the comprehensive nature of the command.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic and Translation
Carefully read the Arabic passage and its literal translation to get the commentator's exact words.
Notice the concise style of the commentary.
2
Identify the Two Commands
Notice how the commentator explains each part of the verse: 'do what is permitted' and 'leave what is not permitted'.
Each command is treated separately.
3
Understand the Definition of 'Permitted'
The commentator defines 'permitted' as 'what is not prohibited', which includes wājib, mandūb, and mubāḥ. Reflect on why this is broader than common understanding.
The permissible (mubāḥ) is defined by equal sides.
4
Understand 'Not Permitted'
The commentator defines 'not permitted' as what is prohibited, including ḥarām, makrūh, and khilāf al-awlā. Note the gradations.
These categories are all forbidden to do.
5
Reflect on the Scope
Consider how this classification impacts one's understanding of Islamic law—every action falls into one of these categories.
This clarifies the verse's practical implications.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (فَمَا أُبِيْحَ افْعَلْ أي : فما لم يُنْهَ عنه، ولو تنزيهاً افعل، فالمراد بما أبيح ما لم ينه عنه، فيشمل الواجب والمندوب والمباح ، وهو ما استوى طرفاه؛ أي: فعله وتركُه . وقوله : (وَدَعْ مَا لَمْ يُبَحْ) أي : واترك ما لم يبح لك فعله؛ وهو المنهي عنه كان مُحرَّماً أو مكروهاً أو خلاف الأولى.
His saying: (So what is permitted, do) means: So what is not prohibited, even if it is repugnant (disliked), do. So what is meant by 'what is permitted' is what is not prohibited; thus it includes the obligatory, the recommended, and the permissible, which is that whose two sides are equal, i.e., doing it and leaving it. And his saying: (And leave what is not permitted) means: And leave what is not permitted for you to do, and it is what is prohibited, whether it is forbidden, disliked, or contrary to what is more appropriate.
Source pages p. 420

Commentary
This passage is a definition of the two commands in the verse: 'So what is permitted, do, and leave what is not permitted.' The commentator explains that 'what is permitted' (mā ubīḥa) means anything not prohibited, even if disliked (tanzīhan). So it includes three categories: obligatory (wājib), recommended (mandūb), and permissible (mubāḥ). He defines the permissible as that whose performance and omission are equal. Then he explains 'leave what is not permitted' (mā lam yubaḥ) as anything that is not permissible to do, which includes the prohibited (ḥarām), the disliked (makrūh), and that which is contrary to what is more appropriate (khilāf al-awlā). The passage thus clarifies the scope of the verse's command.

Key Terms
أبيح
لم ينه عنه
واجب
مندوب
مباح
محرم
مكروه
خلاف الأولى

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Thinking that 'what is permitted' (mā ubīḥa) only refers to the optional permissible (mubāḥ).
Correction: The commentator clarifies that 'what is permitted' includes the obligatory and recommended as well, because it is defined as what is not prohibited.

Checkpoint
What does 'what is permitted' (mā ubīḥa) include according to the commentator?
A) Only the permissible (mubāḥ)
B) The obligatory, recommended, and permissible
C) Only what is not prohibited and not obligatory
D) All actions without exception

Key Takeaway
The verse's command to do what is permitted actually commands us to do everything that is not prohibited, covering obligatory, recommended, and permissible acts.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you consider the command to 'do what is permitted,' do you typically think only of optional good deeds, or do you recognize that it includes your daily obligations like the five prayers? How might this shift your intention when performing them?
?
The lesson teaches that leaving the not-permitted includes not only major sins but also disliked actions. Is there a disliked habit you have been tolerating? What concrete step can you take today to leave it for Allah's sake?
?
Reflect on the category of 'permissible'—actions whose doing and leaving are equal. Are there permissible activities in your life that you have turned into obligations or prohibitions? How can you restore balance and follow the true ruling?

Follow the Righteous Predecessors
Explains the command to follow the righteous Salaf and defines 'righteous'.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the command to follow the righteous among the Salaf and what 'righteous' means in this context.

Core Idea
Following the righteous Salaf secures authentic belief and practice.

The commentator begins by unpacking the first part of the verse: 'So follow the righteous from those who have passed.' He explains that this means following them in beliefs, words, and deeds, and supports it with the Prophet's command to cling to his sunna and that of the rightly-guided caliphs. This lesson explores the definition of 'righteous' and its significance.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on following the righteous
Read the Arabic passage and its translation, focusing on the phrase 'follow the righteous' and the accompanying hadith.
Notice the metaphor 'bite onto it with your molar teeth' – it emphasizes firm adherence.
2
Understand the definition of 'righteous'
The commentator defines 'righteous' as one who fulfills the rights of Allah and the rights of people. Note that he describes this as very rare.
The term 'righteous' applies to both prophets and saints, but prophets are more complete.
3
Connect to your own practice
Reflect on how this definition challenges you to balance duties toward Allah and toward others.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (فَتَابِع الصَّالِحَ مِمَّنْ سَلَفَا) أي: فتابع في عقائدك وأقوالك وأفعالك الفريق الصالح ممن سلف، كقوله عليه الصلاة والسلام عليكم بسنتي وسنة الخلفاء الراشدين من بعدي، عَضُّوا عليها بالنواجذه ، وهذا كناية عن شدَّة التمسك بها، والصالح: هو القائم بحقوق الله وحقوق العباد ، وهذا أندر من الكبريت الأحمر، ويُطلق الصالح على النبي كما يطلق على الولي، إلا أن الصلاح في الأنبياء أكمل منه في الأولياء. قوله : (وَجَانِبِ البِدْعَةَ مِمَّنْ خَلَفَا :أي واترك البدعة المذمومة ممن جاء بعد خواص الصحابة وعلمائهم . وقد علمت أن البدعة تعتريها الأحكام الخمسة . والحاصل أن كل ما وافق الكتاب والسنة أو الإجماع أو القياس فهو سُنَّةٌ. وما خرج عن ذلك فهو بدعة مذمومةٌ.
His saying: (So follow the righteous from those who have passed) means: So follow in your beliefs, sayings, and actions the righteous group from those who have passed, as he, upon him be peace and blessings, said: 'You must adhere to my sunna and the sunna of the rightly-guided caliphs after me; bite onto it with your molar teeth,' and this is a metaphor for intense clinging to it. And the righteous (al-ṣāliḥ) is the one who fulfills the rights of Allah and the rights of the servants, and this is rarer than red sulfur. And the term 'righteous' is applied to the prophet just as it is applied to the saint, but the righteousness of the prophets is more complete than that of the saints. His saying: (And avoid innovation from those who came after) means: And leave the blameworthy innovation from those who came after the elite of the Companions and their scholars. And you have already learned that innovation is subject to the five rulings. The conclusion is that everything that agrees with the Book, the Sunna, consensus, or analogy is sunna (established practice). And whatever deviates from that is blameworthy innovation.
Source pages p. 421

Commentary
This passage explains two commands: 'Follow the righteous from those who have passed' and 'Avoid innovation from those who came after.' The commentator first interprets following the righteous as adhering to their beliefs, words, and deeds, citing the hadith about clinging to the sunna of the Prophet and the caliphs. He defines 'righteous' as one who fulfills duties toward Allah and people, noting its rarity. He also notes that the term 'righteous' applies to prophets and saints, but prophets are more complete. Then he explains 'avoid innovation' as rejecting blameworthy innovation that appeared after the Companions and their scholars. He reminds that innovation has five rulings (as previously explained). The key principle: whatever aligns with the Qur'an, Sunna, consensus, or analogy is sunna; whatever deviates is blameworthy innovation.

Key Terms
تابع
الصالح
السلف
سنة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think 'righteous' refers only to personal piety without social obligations.
Correction: The commentator explicitly includes 'rights of the servants,' showing that righteousness encompasses both duties toward Allah and toward others.

Checkpoint
According to the commentator, what does 'righteous' mean?
A) One who prays and fasts regularly
B) One who fulfills the rights of Allah and the rights of the servants
C) One who is a scholar of hadith
D) One who follows the caliphs only

Key Takeaway
True righteousness balances duties toward Allah and toward people, and emulating the righteous Salaf is the path to authentic faith.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

Avoid Blameworthy Innovation
Defines blameworthy innovation and provides the criterion for sunna vs innovation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand what constitutes blameworthy innovation and the criterion for distinguishing it from sunna.

Core Idea
Blameworthy innovation is whatever deviates from the Qur'an, Sunna, consensus, or analogy.

The commentator now addresses the second command: 'Avoid innovation from those who came after.' He clarifies that the innovation to be avoided is the blameworthy kind, and he provides a clear yardstick: anything that agrees with the four sources of Islamic law is sunna; anything that contradicts them is blameworthy innovation. This lesson unpacks that principle.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on avoiding innovation
Read the Arabic and translation, noting the definition of 'innovation' and the reference to the five rulings.
The commentator assumes prior knowledge that innovation can be obligatory, recommended, etc.
2
Identify the four criteria for sunna
The passage lists four sources: the Book (Qur'an), the Sunna, consensus (ijma'), and analogy (qiyas). Anything aligning with these is sunna.
3
Contrast with innovation
Anything that deviates from these four sources is blameworthy innovation. Consider how this applies to practices not found in the early generations.
The key is agreement with the sources, not antiquity alone.

Meaning of Hope and Transition in the Poem
Commentator explains grammar, transition, and definition of hope with quotes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the literary transition in the poem and the true meaning of hope (rajā') as requiring action and taking means.

Core Idea
True hope is not passive wishful thinking; it demands effort and sincerity.

The commentator unpacks the poet's phrase 'this' and his turning to hope in Allah. He explains that the poem shifts from warning against innovation to encouraging hope, but warns that hope without action is blameworthy. Through linguistic analysis and poetic examples, he shows that genuine hope must be accompanied by taking means.

Guided Path
1
Parse 'hādhā' and understand the transition
Read how the commentator explains the word 'hādhā' as either an object of an omitted verb or a subject with omitted predicate. Notice that this marks a transition (takhallus) from the previous topic to the new topic of hope.
Transition (takhallus) is a common literary device in classical Arabic poetry.
2
Define true hope (rajā') vs. blameworthy desire (ṭama')
The commentator defines rajā' as attachment of the heart to a desired good while taking the means. Without taking means, it is simply ṭama' (blameworthy covetousness).
This distinction is crucial for understanding Islamic theology of hope.
3
Analyze the supporting quotes
Read the quotes from Ibn al-Jawzī and Ibn al-Mubārak. They illustrate that hoping for reward without effort is like expecting a harvest without planting or a ship to sail on dry land.
These analogies reinforce the necessity of action.
4
Reflect on the lesson's implication
Consider how this understanding of hope affects your own practice: hoping for Allah's mercy must be coupled with obedience and avoidance of sin.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (هذا) مفعول المحذوف؛ أي: افهم هذا، أو مبتدأ والخبر محذوف، والتقدير هذا الذي ذكرته في هذه المنظومة مذهب أهل السنة، أو نحو ذلك. وهذا من باب التخلص؛ وهو الانتقال من غرض - وهو هنا الأمر بمتابعة السلف الصالح، ومجانبة البدعة ممن خلف - إلى غرض آخر - وهو هنا رجاء الإخلاص، وما ذكر بعده - وبين الغرضين تناسب قوله : (هَذَا وَأَرْجُو الله ) الرجاء بالمد : هو تعلق القلب بمرغوب فيه مع الأخذ في الأسباب، وإلا فهو طمع مذموم. قال ابن الجوزي: مثل الراجي مع الإصرار على المعصية، كمثل من رجا حصاداً وما زرع أو ولداً وما نكح ، وقال عبد الله بن المبارك: [من البسيط] مَا بَالُ دِينِكَ تَرْضَى أَنْ تُدَنْسَهُ وثَوْبُكَ الدَّهْرَ مَنْسُولٌ مِنَ الدَّنَسِ؟ تَرْجُو النَّجَاةَ وَلَمْ تَسْلُكْ طَرِيْقَتَها إِنَّ السَّفِينَةَ لَا تَجْرِي عَلَى البَبَسِ
His saying: (This) is an object of an omitted verb, i.e., understand this, or it is a subject with the predicate omitted, and the estimation is: This that I have mentioned in this poem is the creed of the People of the Sunna, or something similar. And this is of the type of transition (takhalluṣ), which is moving from one topic - here the command to follow the righteous predecessors and avoid innovation from those who came after - to another topic - here the hope of sincerity, and what is mentioned after it. And between the two topics there is suitability. His saying: (This and I hope in Allah). Al-Rajā' (hope) with the mad letter is: the attachment of the heart to a desired thing while taking the means, otherwise it is blameworthy covetousness. Ibn al-Jawzī said: The example of one who hopes while persisting in sin is like one who hopes for a harvest without sowing, or a child without marriage. And 'Abd Allāh ibn al-Mubārak said (in basīṭ meter): 'What is the matter with your religion that you accept to defile it, while your garment is always threadbare from filth? You hope for salvation but do not take its path; indeed the ship does not sail on dry land.'
Source pages p. 421

Commentary
This passage is an explanation of the word 'hādhā' (this) and the phrase 'wa-arjū Allāh' (and I hope in Allah) in the poem. The commentator begins by parsing 'hādhā' as either an object of an omitted verb ('understand this') or a subject with an omitted predicate ('this is the creed of Ahl al-Sunna'). He then explains that this is a literary transition (takhalluṣ) from the previous topic (following the Salaf and avoiding innovation) to the new topic (hope for sincerity). Then he defines true hope (rajā') as the heart's attachment to a desired good while taking the necessary means; otherwise, it is blameworthy greed (ṭama'). He supports this with two quotes: Ibn al-Jawzī compares a hopeful but unrepentant sinner to someone hoping for a harvest without planting, and Ibn al-Mubārak's verse likens hoping for salvation without effort to expecting a ship to sail on dry land. Thus, the passage emphasizes that genuine hope must be accompanied by action and avoidance of sin.

Key Terms
تخلص
رجاء
طمع
أسباب
إخلاص

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that hoping for Allah's mercy is enough without any effort or change in behavior.
Correction: The passage clarifies that true hope (rajā') requires taking the means and avoiding sin; without that, it is blameworthy covetousness (ṭama').

Checkpoint
How does the commentator distinguish true hope (rajā') from blameworthy covetousness (ṭama')?
A) True hope is confident, while covetousness is doubtful
B) True hope involves taking the means, while covetousness does not
C) True hope is for this world, covetousness for the afterlife
D) There is no difference

Key Takeaway
True hope in Allah must be accompanied by sincere effort and avoidance of sin.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Where in your life are you hoping for a harvest but refusing to sow? What sin or laziness is blocking the means, and will you abandon it today?
?
Ibn al-Mubārak asks: 'What is the matter with your religion that you accept to defile it?' Where have you become comfortable with spiritual defilement, hoping that Allah will overlook it without you removing it?
?
You say you hope for Jannah and forgiveness. But by neglecting the means—prayer, repentance, avoiding sin—are you actually committing blameworthy greed? What is one specific means you will take today to make your hope true?

The Meaning and Transition of 'هذا' in Supplication
Al-Bajuri analyzes the grammar of 'هذا' as a transition.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the grammatical options for the word 'هذا' in the verse and how it functions as a transition (takhallus) to a new supplication.

Core Idea
The single word 'هذا' bears multiple grammatical possibilities, all pointing to a transition in the poem's supplication.

The commentator begins by analyzing the word 'هذا' (this) that appears at the start of the verse. This word is a key grammatical pivot in the line, and understanding its possible roles helps us grasp the shift in topic. The commentary explains that it could be a subject, a predicate, or an object, and that it marks a literary transition known as takhallus.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three grammatical roles
Read the Arabic passage and identify the three grammatical roles proposed for 'هذا': subject, predicate, or object of an omitted verb.
The passage explicitly lists three options.
2
Understand takhallus
Notice that the passage calls this a 'takhallus' and recall what that means from earlier lessons as a literary device for transition.
Takhallus is common in Arabic poetry.
3
Reflect on the purpose
Consider why a poet might use such a transition and how it affects the supplication, especially the shift to asking for proof.

١٤٢ - هَذَا وَأَرْجُو اللهَ أَنْ يَمْنَحَنا عِنْدَ السُّؤَالِ مُطلَقاً حُجَّتَنَا قوله : (هَذَا) مبتدأ والخبر محذوف أو بالعكس؛ أي: هذا مطلوبي أو المطلوب هذا، أو مفعول لمحذوف؛ أي: اسأل هذا أو نحو ذلك، وهذا من باب التخلص، كما مر مر في نظيره . قوله : (وَأَرْجُو الله لا يخفى أن التعبير بالمضارع يُشعر بالتجدد، فالمعنى: وأرجو الله رجاء متجدداً بتجدد الأحوال والأزمنة والأمكنة. وقوله أَنْ يَمْنَحَنا) أي يعطينا، يُقال : مَنَحَهُ إذا أعطاه . والمنحة العَطِية، و(نا) هو المفعول الأول، و (حجتنا) هو الثاني ؛ لأن هذا الفعل يتعدى لمفعولين. والأولى بمقام الدعاء أن يكون المراد بالضمير الذي هو المفعولُ الأول معاشر المسلمين أو أهل العلم ؛ لحديث : إذا دَعوتم الله فاجمعوا، فلعل فيمن تجمعون مَنْ تنالون بَرَكَتَهُ ويحتمل أن المراد به خصوص الناظم، ويكون تعبيره بضمير العظمة، حيث قال : (يمنحنا)، ولم يقل : (يمنحني)، لإظهار سبب العظمة؛ وهو تأهيل الله إياه لطلب الدعاء أو لطلب العلم تَحَدُّثاً بالنعمة؛ قال تعالى: ﴿وَأَمَّا بِنِعْمَةِ رَبِّكَ فَحَدِتْ ﴾ [الضحى : ١١] ، وهذا لا ينافي أنه مُتذلّل مُتخاضع لمولاه، فلا يَرِدُ أن مقام الدعاء مقامُ ذِلَّةٍ وخضوع، والعظمة تنافي ذلك. وقوله : (عِنْدَ السُّؤَالِ مُطلَقاً ) أي : عند ورود السؤال علينا من الغير حال كونِ السؤال مطلقاً ؛ أي: في الدنيا وفي القبر وفي القيامة كما يُفهم ذلك من المقام، وإن لم يُفَسِّر الإطلاق هنا سابقٌ ولا لاحق. وقول العلماء الإطلاقُ يُفسِّرُهُ سابق أو لاحق أمرٌ أغلبي، كما قاله بعض المحققين . وقوله : (حُجَّتَنَا ) :أي ما نحتج به على جواب (۱) ذلك السؤال احتجاجاً صحيحاً شرعيًّا، بحيث لا طعن فيه ولا امتناع من قبوله . قال بعضُ العارفين : من لُطْفِ (۲) مَنْح الله الحُجَّةَ للإنسان عند السؤال قوله تعالى: أَيُّهَا الْإِنسَنُ مَا غَرَكَ بِرَبِّكَ الكَرِيرِ الانفطار : ٦] ، فإنه ألهمه الحُجَّة بأن يقول : غَرَّني كرمُكَ يا رَبُّ. (۱) قوله : (أي: ما نحتج به على جواب إلخ ) هذا ظاهر في السؤال الوارد في الدنيا ؛ لأن السائل في الدنيا قد يطلب دليلاً على الجواب، وأما جواب السؤال الوارد في الآخرة فاحتياجه إلى دليل يدل عليه غير ظاهر ؛ لأنه لم يرد أن الملائكة يطلبون من الميت بعد جوابه دليلاً يثبت به جوابه، بل متى وفقه الله ، وأجابهم انصرفوا عنه، وقالوا له : نَم نومة العروس، فكان الظاهر حمل الحجة على نفس الجواب. هذا ما ظهر . انتهى أجهوري. (۲) قوله : (من لطف) عبارة الأمير من لطيف. انتهى أجهوري. (۳) لم نجده بهذا اللفظ. وقال الحافظ العراقي في تخريج الإحياء» (٣٠٧/١): لم أجده بهذا اللفظ مرفوعاً، بل هو موقوف على أبي الدرداء.
142 – This, and I hope that God will grant us, at the questioning absolutely, our proof. His saying: (هَذَا) is a subject and the predicate is omitted or vice versa; i.e., this is my request or the requested is this, or it is an object of an omitted verb; i.e., ask this or something similar. And this is a transition (takhallus) as has previously occurred in its like. His saying: (وَأَرْجُو الله) it is not hidden that the use of the imperfect tense indicates renewal, so the meaning: I hope for God a renewed hope with the renewal of circumstances, times, and places. And His saying: (أَنْ يَمْنَحَنا) meaning give us; one says 'manahahu' when he gives him. And 'al-minhah' is a gift. And (نا) is the first object, and (حجتنا) is the second, because this verb takes two objects. The preferable thing in the context of supplication is that the intended referent of the pronoun which is the first object is the community of Muslims or the people of knowledge, because of the hadith: 'When you supplicate God, gather together, perhaps among those you gather there is one through whose blessing you benefit.' And it is probable that the intended referent is specifically the author himself, and his expression with the pronoun of majesty, where he said (يمنحنا) and did not say (يمنحني), is to show the cause of majesty, which is God's enabling him to request supplication or to request knowledge, as a recounting of the favor; Allah said: ﴿وَأَمَّا بِنِعْمَةِ رَبِّكَ فَحَدِّثْ﴾ [Ad-Duha: 11]. And this does not contradict that he is humble and submissive to his Lord, so it cannot be objected that the station of supplication is a station of lowliness and submission, and majesty contradicts that. And His saying: (عِنْدَ السُّؤَالِ مُطلَقاً) meaning: at the occurrence of the question from others to us, the question being absolute; i.e., in this world, in the grave, and on the Day of Resurrection, as is understood from the context, even though no preceding or following context has explained the absoluteness here. And the scholars' statement that absoluteness is explained by a preceding or following context is a majority matter, as some researchers have said. And His saying: (حُجَّتَنَا) i.e., what we argue with in answering that question, a sound legal argument, such that there is no criticism of it nor refusal to accept it. Some gnostics said: Among the kindnesses of God's granting the argument to a person at the time of questioning is His saying: 'O man, what deceived you concerning your Generous Lord?' [Al-Infitar: 6] – for He inspired him the argument by saying: 'Your generosity deceived me, O Lord.' (1) His saying: (i.e., what we argue with in answering etc.) This is apparent regarding the question that occurs in this world, because the questioner in this world may request a proof for the answer. As for answering the question that occurs in the Hereafter, the need for a proof indicating it is not apparent, because it has not been reported that the angels ask the deceased after his answer for a proof to establish his answer; rather, when God grants him success and he answers them, they depart from him and say to him: 'Sleep the sleep of a bride.' So the apparent meaning is to interpret 'al-hujjah' as the answer itself. This is what appears. End of [al-Ujhuri]. (2) His saying: (from kindness) the expression of al-Amir is 'from a kind one'. End of al-Ujhuri. (3) We did not find it with this wording. Al-Hafiz al-`Iraqi said in Takhrij al-Ihya' (1/307): I did not find it with this wording as marfu`, rather it is a mawquf report from Abu al-Darda'.
Source pages p. 425

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on a verse (142) of the poem. The commentator, al-Bajuri, provides a detailed grammatical and theological explanation. He begins by analyzing the word 'هذا' (this) as either a subject with an omitted predicate or an object of an omitted verb, marking a transition (تخلص) to a new topic. Then he explains 'أرجو الله' using the imperfect tense to indicate continuous hope that renews with changing circumstances. The verb 'يمنحنا' (grant us) takes two objects: 'نا' (us) and 'حجتنا' (our proof). The commentator discusses whether the plural pronoun 'نا' refers to the community of Muslims or to the author himself (as a majestic plural). He prefers the former based on the hadith about gathering in supplication, but also allows the latter as showing God's favor. He cites the Quranic verse 'وأما بنعمة ربك فحدث'. Then he clarifies 'عند السؤال مطلقا' (at the questioning absolutely) meaning in the three realms: this world, the grave, and the Hereafter. He notes that the absolute qualification here is not explained by context, contrary to the usual rule. Next, he explains 'حجتنا' as the argument used to answer the question, a sound shari proof. He relates a story that some gnostics found God's kindness in the verse 'ما غرك بربك الكريم' because it inspires the answer 'غرني كرمك يا رب'. The commentary then includes three footnotes: first, a clarification that the proof is only needed in this world, not in the grave; second, a correction of a phrase; third, a verification of the hadith about prayer not being rejected between two prayers. Footnote (3) attributes the hadith to Abu al-Darda' as a mawquf report. The passage follows the order of the verse line by line.

What does the commentator call the literary device used in this verse for transitioning to the supplication?

Method of Learning
intermediate
6 min
The Imperfect Verb and the Object of Supplication
Al-Bajuri explains the continuous nature of hope and the dual-object verb with pronominal scope.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the commentator emphasizes the imperfect tense for continuous hope and how the verb 'يمنح' takes two objects, with discussion on the pronoun 'نا'.

Core Idea
The imperfect tense 'أرجو' signals ongoing renewal of hope, while 'يمنحنا' raises the question of who is included in the supplication.

The commentator now focuses on the verb 'أرجو' (I hope) and its implications. By using the imperfect tense, the poet indicates that his hope is constantly renewed with changing times and circumstances. Then he examines 'يمنحنا' (grant us), noting that it takes two objects and that the pronoun 'نا' (us) could refer to the whole community or be a majestic plural for the author himself.

Guided Path
1
Understand the imperfect tense for renewal
Read the passage about the imperfect tense and understand why it indicates renewal of hope with changing conditions.
The commentator explicitly states that the imperfect gives a sense of renewal.
2
Note the dual-object verb
Observe that 'يمنحنا' takes two objects: 'نا' and 'حجتنا'. This is a grammatical point about the verb 'منح'.
The verb 'منح' is known to take two objects.
3
Examine the pronoun interpretations
Consider the two interpretations of 'نا': either the community of Muslims (based on hadith) or a majestic plural for the author (supported by Quran 93:11).
The commentator prefers the community interpretation but does not exclude the other.

142 – This, and I hope that God will grant us, at the questioning absolutely, our proof. His saying: (هَذَا) is a subject and the predicate is omitted or vice versa; i.e., this is my request or the requested is this, or it is an object of an omitted verb; i.e., ask this or something similar. Commentary
This passage is a commentary on a verse (142) of the poem. The commentator, al-Bajuri, provides a detailed grammatical and theological explanation. He begins by analyzing the word 'هذا' (this) as either a subject with an omitted predicate or an object of an omitted verb, marking a transition (تخلص) to a new topic. Then he explains 'أرجو الله' using the imperfect tense to indicate continuous hope that renews with changing circumstances. The verb 'يمنحنا' (grant us) takes two objects: 'نا' (us) and 'حجتنا' (our proof). The commentator discusses whether the plural pronoun 'نا' refers to the community of Muslims or to the author himself (as a majestic plural). He prefers the former based on the hadith about gathering in supplication, but also allows the latter as showing God's favor. He cites the Quranic verse 'وأما بنعمة ربك فحدث'. Then he clarifies 'عند السؤال مطلقا' (at the questioning absolutely) meaning in the three realms: this world, the grave, and the Hereafter. He notes that the absolute qualification here is not explained by context, contrary to the usual rule. Next, he explains 'حجتنا' as the argument used to answer the question, a sound shari proof. He relates a story that some gnostics found God's kindness in the verse 'ما غرك بربك الكريم' because it inspires the answer 'غرني كرمك يا رب'. The commentary then includes three footnotes: first, a clarification that the proof is only needed in this world, not in the grave; second, a correction of a phrase; third, a verification of the hadith about prayer not being rejected between two prayers. Footnote (3) attributes the hadith to Abu al-Darda' as a mawquf report. The passage follows the order of the verse line by line.

Checkpoint
According to the commentary, what does the imperfect tense 'أرجو' indicate?
A) Past hope
B) Future hope alone
C) Renewed hope over time
D) None of the above

Key Takeaway
The imperfect tense and the dual-object verb highlight that the supplication for proof is a continuously renewed request that includes either the community or the poet himself.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you hope for Allah's guidance with a hope that is renewed every moment, or do you rely on past spiritual achievements? What would it look like to constantly renew your hope today?
?
When you supplicate, do you habitually think only of yourself, or do you intentionally include the wider Muslim community? How might the hadith about gathering for supplication challenge your individualistic prayer habits?
?
The poet uses the majestic plural 'نا' to recount Allah's blessing of enabling him to seek knowledge. Can you truthfully recount Allah's blessings without feeling a hint of pride? Where is the line between gratitude and arrogance in your own heart?
